<p>The <b>University of Michigan</b> (commonly referred to as <b>Michigan</b>, <b>U-M</b>, <b>UMich</b>, or <b>U of M</b>) is a <a href="/wiki/Public_university" title="Public university">public</a> <a href="/wiki/Research_university" title="Research university" class="mw-redirect">research university</a> located in <a href="/wiki/Ann_Arbor,_Michigan" title="Ann Arbor, Michigan">Ann Arbor, Michigan</a> in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Carnegie_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carnegie-3"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup> It is the state's oldest university and the flagship campus of the University of Michigan. U-M also has satellite campuses in <a href="/wiki/University_of_Michigan%E2%80%93Flint" title="University of Michigan–Flint">Flint</a> and <a href="/wiki/University_of_Michigan%E2%80%93Dearborn" title="University of Michigan–Dearborn">Dearborn</a>.</p>
<p>The university was founded in 1817 in <a href="/wiki/Detroit" title="Detroit">Detroit</a> as the <b>Catholepistemiad</b>, or <b>University of Michigania</b>, about 20 years before the <a href="/wiki/Michigan_Territory" title="Michigan Territory">Michigan Territory</a> officially became a state.<sup id="cite_ref-university_history_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-university_history-4"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup> What would become the university moved to <a href="/wiki/Ann_Arbor,_Michigan" title="Ann Arbor, Michigan">Ann Arbor</a> in 1837 onto 40 acres (16&#160;ha) of what is now known as Central Campus.<sup id="cite_ref-central_campus_history_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-central_campus_history-5"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup> Since its establishment in Ann Arbor, the university has physically expanded to include more than 584 major buildings with a combined area of more than 31 million gross square feet (712&#160;acres or 2.38&#160;km²),<sup id="cite_ref-campus_size_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-campus_size-6"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> and transformed its academic program from a strictly classical curriculum to one that includes science and research.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup> U-M was the site of much <a href="/wiki/Student_activism" title="Student activism">student activism</a> in the 1960s.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup> When presidential candidate <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">John F. Kennedy</a> visited the University on October 14, 1960, he gave an impromptu speech on the steps of the <a href="/wiki/Michigan_Union" title="Michigan Union">Michigan Union</a> that led to a University of Michigan student movement which contributed to the establishment of the <a href="/wiki/Peace_Corps" title="Peace Corps">Peace Corps</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup> The University was also a focal point in the controversy over <a href="/wiki/Affirmative_action" title="Affirmative action">affirmative action</a> within higher education admissions.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The University of Michigan is ranked among the top 5 public universities nationally<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup> and is considered to be one of America's <a href="/wiki/Public_Ivy" title="Public Ivy">Public Ivy</a> universities, which recognizes top public research universities in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup> In the 2011 <i>U.S. News &amp; World Report</i> "National University Rankings", the university was ranked 29th among national universities in the United States,<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1995, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_National_Research_Council" title="United States National Research Council">National Research Council</a> ranked Michigan <a href="/wiki/United_States_National_Research_Council_Rankings" title="United States National Research Council Rankings" class="mw-redirect">third nationally</a> for the quality of its graduate programs. Michigan has one of the world's largest living alumni groups at 460,000 in 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-alumni_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-alumni-15"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup> U-M owns the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Michigan_Health_System" title="University of Michigan Health System">University of Michigan Health System</a><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup> and has one of the largest research expenditures of any American university, passing the $1.24 billion mark during the 2010-2011 academic year.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Its athletic teams, called the <a href="/wiki/Michigan_Wolverines" title="Michigan Wolverines">Wolverines</a>, are members of the <a href="/wiki/Big_Ten_Conference" title="Big Ten Conference">Big Ten Conference</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Central_Collegiate_Hockey_Association" title="Central Collegiate Hockey Association">Central Collegiate Hockey Association</a>. The athletic program is known for its success in <a href="/wiki/Ice_hockey" title="Ice hockey">ice hockey</a> and <a href="/wiki/College_football" title="College football">football</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-football_records_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-football_records-18"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup> The football team plays in <a href="/wiki/Michigan_Stadium" title="Michigan Stadium">Michigan Stadium</a>, also known as "The Big House," the largest football stadium in the world.</p>

<h2><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Michigan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_University_of_Michigan" title="History of the University of Michigan">History of the University of Michigan</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:JasperCropseyUniversityOfMichigan.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Painting of a rolling green landscape with trees with a row of white buildings in the background" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/JasperCropseyUniversityOfMichigan.jpg/220px-JasperCropseyUniversityOfMichigan.jpg" width="220" height="106" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:JasperCropseyUniversityOfMichigan.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.18/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
<i>University of Michigan</i> (1855) by <a href="/wiki/Jasper_Francis_Cropsey" title="Jasper Francis Cropsey">Jasper Francis Cropsey</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The University of Michigan was established in Detroit in 1817 as the <i>Catholepistemiad</i>, or University of Michigania, by the governor and judges of <a href="/wiki/Michigan_Territory" title="Michigan Territory">Michigan Territory</a>. The Rev. John Monteith was one of the university's founders and its first President. Ann Arbor had set aside 40 acres (16&#160;ha) that it hoped would become the site for a new state capitol, but it offered this land to the university when <a href="/wiki/Lansing,_Michigan" title="Lansing, Michigan">Lansing</a> was chosen as the state capital. What would become the university moved to Ann Arbor in 1837 thanks to governor <a href="/wiki/Stevens_T._Mason" title="Stevens T. Mason">Stevens T. Mason</a>. The original 40 acres (160,000 m<sup>2</sup>) became part of the current Central Campus.<sup id="cite_ref-central_campus_history_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-central_campus_history-5"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup> The first classes in Ann Arbor were held in 1841, with six freshmen and a sophomore, taught by two professors. Eleven students graduated in the first commencement in 1845.<sup id="cite_ref-university_history_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-university_history-4"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup> By 1866 enrollment increased to 1,205 students, many of whom were <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">Civil War</a> veterans. Women were first admitted in 1870.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/James_Burrill_Angell" title="James Burrill Angell">James Burrill Angell</a>, who served as the university's president from 1871 to 1909, aggressively expanded U-M's curriculum to include professional studies in <a href="/wiki/Dentistry" title="Dentistry">dentistry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Architecture" title="Architecture">architecture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Engineering" title="Engineering">engineering</a>, <a href="/wiki/Government" title="Government">government</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Medicine" title="Medicine">medicine</a>. U-M also became the first American university to use the <a href="/wiki/Seminar" title="Seminar">seminar</a> method of study.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>From 1900 to 1920 the university constructed many new facilities, including buildings for the dental and pharmacy programs, chemistry, natural sciences, <a href="/wiki/Hill_Auditorium" title="Hill Auditorium">Hill Auditorium</a>, large hospital and library complexes, and two residence halls. In 1920 the university reorganized the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Michigan_College_of_Engineering" title="University of Michigan College of Engineering">College of Engineering</a> and formed an advisory committee of 100 industrialists to guide academic research initiatives. The university became a favored choice for bright Jewish students from New York in the 1920s and 1930s when the <a href="/wiki/Ivy_League" title="Ivy League">Ivy League</a> schools had quotas restricting the number of Jews to be admitted.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup> As a result, U-M gained the nickname "Harvard of the West," which became commonly parodied in reverse after <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">John F. Kennedy</a> referred to himself as "a graduate of the Michigan of the East, Harvard University" in his speech proposing the formation of the <a href="/wiki/Peace_Corps" title="Peace Corps">Peace Corps</a> while on the front steps of the Michigan Union.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup> During <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, U-M's research grew to include <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">U.S. Navy</a> projects such as <a href="/wiki/Proximity_fuze" title="Proximity fuze">proximity fuzes</a>, <a href="/wiki/PT_boat" title="PT boat">PT boats</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Radar_jamming" title="Radar jamming" class="mw-redirect">radar jamming</a>.</p>
<p>By 1950, enrollment had reached 21,000, of whom more than one third: 7,700 were veterans supported by the <a href="/wiki/G.I._Bill" title="G.I. Bill">G.I. Bill</a>. As the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Space_Race" title="Space Race">Space Race</a> took hold, U-M became a major recipient of government grants for strategic research and helped to develop peacetime uses for <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power" title="Nuclear power">nuclear energy</a>. Much of that work, as well as research into alternative energy sources, is pursued via the Memorial Phoenix Project.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:UniversityofMIchiganDiag.JPG" class="image"><img alt="Red brick plaza, surrounded by trees with green leaves, with two white tents and an American flag flying from a flagpole in the center" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/UniversityofMIchiganDiag.JPG/220px-UniversityofMIchiganDiag.JPG" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:UniversityofMIchiganDiag.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.18/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
The Central Campus Diag, viewed from the Graduate Library, looking North</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Lyndon_B._Johnson" title="Lyndon B. Johnson">Lyndon B. Johnson</a>'s speech outlining his <a href="/wiki/Great_Society" title="Great Society">Great Society</a> program was given during U-M's 1964 spring commencement ceremony.<sup id="cite_ref-university_history_4-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-university_history-4"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup> During the 1960s, there were numerous protests against the Vietnam War and related to other issues at the U-M. On March 24, 1965, a group of U-M faculty members and 3,000 students held the nation's first ever faculty-led "teach-in" to protest against American policy in Southeast Asia.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup> In response to a series of <a href="/wiki/Sit-in" title="Sit-in">sit-ins</a> in 1966 by <i>Voice</i>–the campus political party of <a href="/wiki/Students_for_a_Democratic_Society_(1960_organization)" title="Students for a Democratic Society (1960 organization)" class="mw-redirect">Students for a Democratic Society</a>, U-M's administration banned sit-ins. In response, 1,500 students had a one-hour sit-in the LSA Building, which housed administrative offices.</p>
<p>Former U-M student and noted architect <a href="/wiki/Alden_B._Dow" title="Alden B. Dow">Alden B. Dow</a> designed the current Fleming Administration Building, which was completed in 1968. The building's plans were drawn in the early 1960s, before student activism prompted a concern for safety, but the Fleming Building's narrow windows, all located above the first floor, and fortress-like exterior led to a campus rumor that it was designed to be riot-proof. Dow denied those rumors, claiming the small windows were designed to be energy efficient.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>During the 1970s, severe budget constraints challenged the university's physical development; but, in the 1980s, the university received increased grants for research in the social and physical sciences. The university's involvement in the anti-missile <a href="/wiki/Strategic_Defense_Initiative" title="Strategic Defense Initiative">Strategic Defense Initiative</a> and investments in <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a> caused controversy on campus.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup> During the 1980s and 1990s, the university devoted substantial resources to renovating its massive hospital complex and improving the academic facilities on the North Campus. In its 2011 annual financial report, the university announced that it had dedicated $497 million per year in each of the prior 10 years to renovate buildings and infrastructure around the campus. The university also emphasized the development of computer and information technology throughout the campus.</p>
<p>In the early 2000s, U-M also faced declining state funding due to state budget shortfalls. At the same time, the university attempted to maintain its high academic standing while keeping <a href="/wiki/Tuition" title="Tuition" class="mw-redirect">tuition</a> costs affordable. There were disputes between U-M's administration and labor unions, notably with the Lecturers' Employees Organization (LEO) and the Graduate Employees Organization (GEO), the union representing graduate student employees. These conflicts led to a series of one-day walkouts by the unions and their supporters.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup> The university is currently engaged in a $2.5 billion construction campaign.<sup id="cite_ref-Schneider_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schneider-30"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:UMichiganLawLibraryInterior.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Law Library" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/UMichiganLawLibraryInterior.jpg/220px-UMichiganLawLibraryInterior.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:UMichiganLawLibraryInterior.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.18/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
Law Library Interior</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 2003, two lawsuits involving U-M's <a href="/wiki/Affirmative_action_in_the_United_States" title="Affirmative action in the United States">affirmative action</a> admissions policy reached the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">U.S. Supreme Court</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Grutter_v._Bollinger" title="Grutter v. Bollinger">Grutter v. Bollinger</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Gratz_v._Bollinger" title="Gratz v. Bollinger">Gratz v. Bollinger</a></i>). President <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a> took the unusual step of publicly opposing the policy before the court issued a ruling.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup> The court found that race may be considered as a factor in university admissions in all public universities and private universities that accept federal funding. But, it ruled that a point system was unconstitutional. In the first case, the court upheld the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Michigan_Law_School" title="University of Michigan Law School">Law School</a> admissions policy, while in the second it ruled against the university's undergraduate admissions policy.</p>
<p>The debate continues because in November 2006, Michigan voters passed <a href="/wiki/Michigan_Civil_Rights_Initiative" title="Michigan Civil Rights Initiative">Proposal 2</a>, banning most affirmative action in university admissions. Under that law race, gender, and national origin can no longer be considered in admissions.<sup id="cite_ref-NoAffirAct_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NoAffirAct-32"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup> U-M and other organizations were granted a stay from implementation of the passed proposal soon after that election, and this has allowed time for proponents of affirmative action to decide legal and constitutional options in response to the election results. The university has stated it plans to continue to challenge the ruling; in the meantime, the admissions office states that it will attempt to achieve a diverse student body by looking at other factors, such as whether the student attended a disadvantaged school, and the level of education of the student's parents.<sup id="cite_ref-NoAffirAct_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NoAffirAct-32"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Michigan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Campus">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Campus">Campus</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:UMich_campus_locations_(Ann_Arbor).png" class="image"><img alt="Black outline of city with three blues blocks in the center, one cyan block in the center, and one cyan block at the bottom inside the black outline" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/UMich_campus_locations_%28Ann_Arbor%29.png/200px-UMich_campus_locations_%28Ann_Arbor%29.png" width="200" height="195" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:UMich_campus_locations_(Ann_Arbor).png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.18/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
Locations of the three main U-M campuses in Ann Arbor.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Ann Arbor campus is divided into four main areas: the North, Central, Medical, and South Campuses. The physical <a href="/wiki/Infrastructure" title="Infrastructure">infrastructure</a> includes more than 500 major buildings, with a combined area of more than 31 million square feet (712&#160;acres or 2.38&#160;km²).<sup id="cite_ref-campus_size_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-campus_size-6"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> The Central and South Campus areas are contiguous, while the North Campus area is separated from them, primarily by the <a href="/wiki/Huron_River_(Michigan)" title="Huron River (Michigan)">Huron River</a>. There is also leased space in buildings scattered throughout the city, many occupied by organizations affiliated with the University of Michigan Health System. An East Medical Campus has recently been developed on Plymouth Road, with several university-owned buildings for outpatient care, diagnostics, and outpatient surgery.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In addition to the U-M Golf Course on South Campus, the university operates a second golf course called "Radrick Farms Golf Course" on Geddes Road. The golf course is only open to faculty, staff, and alumni.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup> Another off-campus facility is the Inglis House, which the university has owned since the 1950s. The Inglis House is a 10,000-square-foot (930&#160;m<sup>2</sup>) mansion used to hold various social events, including meetings of the board of regents, and to host visiting dignitaries.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup> The university also operates a large office building called Wolverine Tower in southern Ann Arbor near Briarwood Mall. Another major facility is the <a href="/wiki/Matthaei_Botanical_Gardens" title="Matthaei Botanical Gardens">Matthaei Botanical Gardens</a>, which is located on the eastern outskirts of Ann Arbor.</p>
<p>All four campus areas are connected by bus services, the majority of which connect the North and Central Campuses. There is a shuttle service connecting the University Hospital, which lies between North and Central Campuses, with other medical facilities throughout northeastern Ann Arbor.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Michigan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Central Campus">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Central_Campus">Central Campus</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hill_Auditorium_2010.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Red brick building with white stone facade. A tall white-colored stone clock tower with a green roof is in the background" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Hill_Auditorium_2010.jpg/220px-Hill_Auditorium_2010.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hill_Auditorium_2010.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.18/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
Hill Auditorium and Burton Tower</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Central Campus was the original location of U-M when it moved to Ann Arbor in 1837. It originally had a school and dormitory building (where Mason Hall now stands) and several houses for professors on forty acres of land bounded by North University Avenue, South University Avenue, East University Avenue, and State Street. The President's House, located on South University Avenue, is the oldest building on campus as well as the only surviving building from the original forty acre campus.<sup id="cite_ref-central_campus_history_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-central_campus_history-5"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup> Because Ann Arbor and Central Campus developed simultaneously, there is no distinct boundary between the city and university, and some areas contain a mixture of private and university buildings.</p>
<p>Central Campus is the location of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Michigan_College_of_Literature,_Science,_and_the_Arts" title="University of Michigan College of Literature, Science, and the Arts">College of Literature, Science and the Arts</a>, and is immediately adjacent to the medical campus. Most of the graduate and professional schools, including the <a href="/wiki/Ross_School_of_Business" title="Ross School of Business">Ross School of Business</a>, the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Michigan_Law_School" title="University of Michigan Law School">Law School</a> and the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Michigan_School_of_Dentistry" title="University of Michigan School of Dentistry">School of Dentistry</a>, are on Central Campus. Two prominent libraries, the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Michigan_Library#Harlan_Hatcher_Graduate_Library" title="University of Michigan Library">Harlan Hatcher Graduate Library</a> and the Shapiro Undergraduate Library which are connected by a <a href="/wiki/Skyway" title="Skyway">skywalk</a>, are also on Central Campus, as well as <a href="/wiki/Museums_at_the_University_of_Michigan" title="Museums at the University of Michigan">museums</a> housing collections in <a href="/wiki/Archaeology" title="Archaeology">archaeology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anthropology" title="Anthropology">anthropology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paleontology" title="Paleontology">paleontology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zoology" title="Zoology">zoology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dentistry" title="Dentistry">dentistry</a>, and art. Ten of the buildings on Central Campus were designed by Detroit-based architect <a href="/wiki/Albert_Kahn_(architect)" title="Albert Kahn (architect)">Albert Kahn</a> between 1904 and 1936. The most notable of the Kahn-designed buildings are the <a href="/wiki/Burton_Tower" title="Burton Tower" class="mw-redirect">Burton Memorial Tower</a> and nearby <a href="/wiki/Hill_Auditorium" title="Hill Auditorium">Hill Auditorium</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Michigan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: North Campus">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="North_Campus">North Campus</span></h3>
<p>North Campus is the most contiguous campus, built independently from the city on a large plot of farm land—approximately 800 acres (3.2&#160;km<sup>2</sup>)—that the university bought in 1952.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup> It is newer than Central Campus, and thus has more <a href="/wiki/Modern_architecture" title="Modern architecture">modern architecture</a>, whereas most Central Campus buildings are classical or gothic in style. The architect <a href="/wiki/Eero_Saarinen" title="Eero Saarinen">Eero Saarinen</a>, based in <a href="/wiki/Birmingham,_Michigan" title="Birmingham, Michigan">Birmingham, Michigan</a>, created one of the early master plans for North Campus and designed several of its buildings in the 1950s, including the Earl V. Moore School of Music Building.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup> North and Central Campuses each have unique bell towers that reflect the predominant architectural styles of their surroundings. Each of the bell towers houses a grand <a href="/wiki/Carillon" title="Carillon">carillon</a>. The North Campus tower is called <a href="/wiki/Lurie_Tower" title="Lurie Tower">Lurie Tower</a>. The University of Michigan's largest residence hall, Bursley Hall, is located on North Campus.</p>
<p>North Campus houses the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Michigan_College_of_Engineering" title="University of Michigan College of Engineering">College of Engineering</a>, the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Michigan_School_of_Music" title="University of Michigan School of Music" class="mw-redirect">School of Music, Theatre &amp; Dance</a>, the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Michigan_School_of_Art_%26_Design" title="University of Michigan School of Art &amp; Design">School of Art &amp; Design</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Taubman_College_of_Architecture_and_Urban_Planning" title="Taubman College of Architecture and Urban Planning">Taubman College of Architecture and Urban Planning</a>, and an annex of the School of Information. The campus is served by the Duderstadt Center, which houses the Art, Architecture and Engineering Library. The Duderstadt Center also contains multiple <a href="/wiki/Computer_lab" title="Computer lab">computer labs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Non-linear_editing_system" title="Non-linear editing system">video editing</a> studios, electronic music studios, an audio studio, a video studio, multimedia workspaces, and a <a href="/wiki/3D_computer_graphics" title="3D computer graphics">3D</a> <a href="/wiki/Virtual_reality" title="Virtual reality">virtual reality</a> room. Other libraries located on North Campus include the <a href="/wiki/Gerald_R._Ford_Presidential_Library" title="Gerald R. Ford Presidential Library">Gerald R. Ford Presidential Library</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Bentley_Historical_Library" title="Bentley Historical Library">Bentley Historical Library</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Michigan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: South Campus">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="South_Campus">South Campus</span></h3>
<p>South Campus is the site for the athletic programs, including major sports facilities such as <a href="/wiki/Michigan_Stadium" title="Michigan Stadium">Michigan Stadium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crisler_Arena" title="Crisler Arena">Crisler Arena</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Yost_Ice_Arena" title="Yost Ice Arena">Yost Ice Arena</a>. South Campus is also the site of the Buhr library storage facility (the collections of which are undergoing digitization by Google), the Institute for Continuing Legal Education, and the Student Theatre Arts Complex, which provides shop and rehearsal space for student theatre groups. The university's departments of public safety and transportation services offices are located on South Campus.</p>
<p>U-M's golf course is located south of Michigan Stadium and Crisler Arena. It was designed in the late 1920s by <a href="/wiki/Alister_MacKenzie" title="Alister MacKenzie">Alister MacKenzie</a>, the designer of <a href="/wiki/Augusta_National_Golf_Club" title="Augusta National Golf Club">Augusta National Golf Club</a> in <a href="/wiki/Augusta,_Georgia" title="Augusta, Georgia">Augusta, Georgia</a> (home of The <a href="/wiki/Masters_Tournament" title="Masters Tournament">Masters Tournament</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup> The course opened to the public in the spring of 1931. The University of Michigan Golf Course was included in a listing of top holes designed by what <i><a href="/wiki/Sports_Illustrated" title="Sports Illustrated">Sports Illustrated</a></i> calls “golf’s greatest course architect.” The U-M Golf Course’s signature No. 6 hole—a 310-yard (280&#160;m) par 4, which plays from an elevated tee to a two-tiered, kidney-shaped green protected by four bunkers—is the second hole on the Alister MacKenzie Dream 18 as selected by a five-person panel that includes three-time Masters champion <a href="/wiki/Nick_Faldo" title="Nick Faldo">Nick Faldo</a> and golf course architect <a href="/wiki/Tom_Doak" title="Tom Doak">Tom Doak</a>. The listing of “the best holes ever designed by Augusta National architect Alister MacKenzie” is featured in SI’s Golf Plus special edition previewing the Masters on April 4, 2006.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Michigan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Organization and administration">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Organization_and_administration">Organization and administration</span></h2>
<div class="rellink boilerplate seealso">See also: <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_University_of_Michigan" title="President of the University of Michigan">President of the University of Michigan</a>&#160;and <a href="/wiki/Board_of_Regents_of_the_University_of_Michigan" title="Board of Regents of the University of Michigan">Board of Regents of the University of Michigan</a></div>

<td>1984</td>

</table>
<p>The University of Michigan consists of a flagship campus in Ann Arbor, with two regional campuses in <a href="/wiki/University_of_Michigan%E2%80%93Dearborn" title="University of Michigan–Dearborn">Dearborn</a> and <a href="/wiki/University_of_Michigan-Flint" title="University of Michigan-Flint" class="mw-redirect">Flint</a>. The Board of Regents, which governs the university and was established by the Organic Act of March 18, 1837, consists of eight members elected at large in biennial state elections<sup id="cite_ref-Hebel2004_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hebel2004-43"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup> for overlapping eight year terms.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup> Between the establishment of the University of Michigan in 1837 and 1850, the Board of Regents ran the university directly; although they were, by law, supposed to appoint a Chancellor to administer the university, they never did. Instead a rotating roster of professors carried out the day-to-day administration duties.<sup id="cite_ref-Hinsdale1906_37_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hinsdale1906_37-46"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_University_of_Michigan" title="President of the University of Michigan">President of the University of Michigan</a> is the principal executive officer of the university. The office was created by the <a href="/wiki/Michigan_Constitution" title="Michigan Constitution">Michigan Constitution</a> of 1850, which also specified that the president was to be appointed by the Regents of the University of Michigan and preside at their meetings, but without a vote.<sup id="cite_ref-StateOfMichigan1850_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StateOfMichigan1850-47"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup> Today, the president's office is at the Ann Arbor campus, and the president has the privilege of living in the President's House, the university's oldest building located on Central Campus in Ann Arbor.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mary_Sue_Coleman" title="Mary Sue Coleman">Mary Sue Coleman</a> is the 13th president of the university and has served since August 2002.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup> Her compensation for 2008–2009 totaled $783,850.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span>[</span>51<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>There are thirteen undergraduate schools and colleges.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup> By enrollment, the three largest undergraduate units are the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Michigan_College_of_Literature,_Science,_and_the_Arts" title="University of Michigan College of Literature, Science, and the Arts">College of Literature, Science, and the Arts</a>, the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Michigan_College_of_Engineering" title="University of Michigan College of Engineering">College of Engineering</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Ross_School_of_Business" title="Ross School of Business">Ross School of Business</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-size_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-size-52"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup> At the graduate level, the Rackham Graduate School serves as the central administrative unit of graduate education at the university.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup> There are 18 graduate schools and colleges, the largest of which are the College of Literature, Science, and the Arts, the College of Engineering, the Law School, and the Ross School of Business. <a href="/wiki/First_professional_degree" title="First professional degree">Professional degrees</a> are conferred by the Schools of Public Health, <a href="/wiki/University_of_Michigan_School_of_Dentistry" title="University of Michigan School of Dentistry">Dentistry</a>, Law, <a href="/wiki/University_of_Michigan_Medical_School" title="University of Michigan Medical School" class="mw-redirect">Medicine</a>, and Pharmacy.<sup id="cite_ref-size_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-size-52"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup> The Medical School is partnered with the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Michigan_Health_System" title="University of Michigan Health System">University of Michigan Health System</a>, which comprises the university's three hospitals, dozens of outpatient clinics, and many centers for medical care, research, and education.</p>
<h3><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Michigan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Endowment">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Endowment">Endowment</span></h3>
<p>U-M's <a href="/wiki/Financial_endowment" title="Financial endowment">financial endowment</a> (the "University Endowment Fund") was valued at $7.57 billion in NACUBO's 2008 ranking.<sup id="cite_ref-NACUBO_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NACUBO-54"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup> It was the <a href="/wiki/List_of_U.S._colleges_and_universities_by_endowment" title="List of U.S. colleges and universities by endowment" class="mw-redirect">seventh largest</a> endowment in the U.S. and the third-largest among U.S public universities at that time, as well as the <a href="/wiki/List_of_U.S._colleges_and_universities_by_endowment#Certain_institutions_by_endowment_growth" title="List of U.S. colleges and universities by endowment" class="mw-redirect">fastest growing</a> endowment in the nation over the last 21 years.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup> The endowment is primarily used according to the donors' wishes, which include the support of teaching and research. In mid-2000, U-M embarked on a massive fund-raising campaign called "The Michigan Difference," which aimed to raise $2.5 billion, with $800 million designated for the permanent endowment.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup> Slated to run through December 2008, the university announced that the campaign had reached its target 19 months early in May 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup> Ultimately, the campaign raised $3.2 billion over 8 years. Over the course of the capital campaign, 191 additional professorships were endowed, bringing the university total to 471 as of 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup> Like nearly all colleges and universities, U-M suffered significant realized and unrealized losses in its endowment during the second half of 2008. In February 2009, a university spokesperson estimated losses of between 20 and 30 percent.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup> As of June 2010, the endowment was estimated to be $6.7 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60"><span>[</span>61<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Michigan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Student government">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Student_government">Student government</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:AngellHall2010.JPG" class="image"><img alt="White-colored stone building with columns in the center of the facade" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/AngellHall2010.JPG/220px-AngellHall2010.JPG" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:AngellHall2010.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.18/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
Central Campus: Angell Hall, one of the major buildings of the College of Literature, Science, and the Arts</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Housed in the Michigan Union, the <a href="/wiki/Student_governments_in_the_United_States#University_of_Michigan" title="Student governments in the United States">Michigan Student Assembly</a> (MSA) is the central student government of the University. With representatives from each of the University's colleges and schools, the MSA represents students and manages student funds on the campus. In recent years MSA has organized airBus, a transportation service between campus and the <a href="/wiki/Detroit_Metropolitan_Wayne_County_Airport" title="Detroit Metropolitan Wayne County Airport">Detroit Metropolitan Wayne County Airport</a>, and has led the university's efforts to register its student population to vote, with its Voice Your Vote Commission (VYV) registering 10,000 students in 2004. VYV also works to improve access to non-partisan voting-related information and increase student voter turnout.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup> MSA has also been successful at reviving <a href="/wiki/Homecoming" title="Homecoming">Homecoming</a> activities, including a carnival and parade, for students after a roughly eleven-year absence in October 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span>[</span>63<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>There are student governance bodies in each college and school. The two largest colleges at the University of Michigan are the College of Literature, Science, and the Arts (LS&amp;A) and the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Michigan_College_of_Engineering" title="University of Michigan College of Engineering">College of Engineering</a>. Undergraduate students in the LS&amp;A are represented by the LS&amp;A Student Government (LSA SG). The University of Michigan Engineering Council (UMEC) manages undergraduate student government affairs for the College of Engineering. Graduate students enrolled in the Rackham Graduate School are represented by the Rackham Student Government (RSG). In addition, the students that live in the residence halls are represented by the University of Michigan Residence Halls Association.</p>
<p>A longstanding goal of the student government is to create a student-designated seat on the Board of Regents, the university's governing body.<sup id="cite_ref-student_regent_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-student_regent-63"><span>[</span>64<span>]</span></a></sup> Such a designation would achieve parity with other Big Ten schools that have student regents. In 2000, students Nick Waun and Scott Trudeau ran for the board on the state-wide ballot as third-party nominees. Waun ran for a second time in 2002, along with Matt Petering and Susan Fawcett.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64"><span>[</span>65<span>]</span></a></sup> Although none of these campaigns has been successful, a poll conducted by the State of Michigan in 1998 concluded that a majority of Michigan voters would approve of such a position if the measure were put before them.<sup id="cite_ref-student_regent_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-student_regent-63"><span>[</span>64<span>]</span></a></sup> A change to the board's makeup would require amending the <a href="/wiki/Michigan_Constitution" title="Michigan Constitution">Michigan Constitution</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Michigan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Academics">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Academics">Academics</span></h2>
<table class="infobox vcard" style="width: 22em">

<tr>
<th><i><a href="/wiki/Times_Higher_Education_World_University_Rankings" title="Times Higher Education World University Rankings">Times</a></i><sup id="cite_ref-Rankings_THES_W_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rankings_THES_W-72"><span>[</span>73<span>]</span></a></sup></th>
<td>18</td>

<p>The University of Michigan is a large, four-year, residential research university accredited by the <a href="/wiki/North_Central_Association_of_Colleges_and_Schools" title="North Central Association of Colleges and Schools">North Central Association of Colleges and Schools</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Carnegie_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carnegie-3"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73"><span>[</span>74<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74"><span>[</span>75<span>]</span></a></sup> The four year, full-time undergraduate program comprises the majority of enrollments and emphasizes instruction in the arts, sciences, and professions and there is a high level of coexistence between graduate and undergraduate programs. The university has "very high" research activity and the "comprehensive" graduate program offers doctoral degrees in the humanities, social sciences, and <a href="/wiki/STEM_fields" title="STEM fields">STEM fields</a> as well as professional degrees in medicine, law, and dentistry.<sup id="cite_ref-Carnegie_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carnegie-3"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup> U-M has been included on <a href="/wiki/Richard_Moll" title="Richard Moll">Richard Moll</a>'s list of <a href="/wiki/Public_Ivies" title="Public Ivies" class="mw-redirect">Public Ivies</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75"><span>[</span>76<span>]</span></a></sup> With over 200 undergraduate majors, 100 doctoral and 90 master's programs,<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76"><span>[</span>77<span>]</span></a></sup> U-M conferred 6,473 undergraduate degrees, 4,322 graduate degrees, and 734 first professional degrees in 2008-2009.<sup id="cite_ref-Conferred_Degrees_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Conferred_Degrees-77"><span>[</span>78<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>National honor societies such as <a href="/wiki/Phi_Beta_Kappa" title="Phi Beta Kappa" class="mw-redirect">Phi Beta Kappa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phi_Kappa_Phi" title="Phi Kappa Phi">Phi Kappa Phi</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tau_Beta_Pi" title="Tau Beta Pi">Tau Beta Pi</a> have chapters at U-M.<sup id="cite_ref-Honors_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Honors-78"><span>[</span>79<span>]</span></a></sup> Degrees "with Highest Distinction" are recommended to students who rank in the top 3% of their class, "with High Distinction" to the next 7%, and "with Distinction" to the next 15%. Students earning a minimum overall GPA of 3.4 who have demonstrated high academic achievement and capacity for independent work may be recommended for a degree "with Highest Honors," "with High Honors," or "with Honors."<sup id="cite_ref-Honors_78-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Honors-78"><span>[</span>79<span>]</span></a></sup> Those students who earn all A's for two or more consecutive terms in a calendar year are recognized as <a href="/wiki/James_B._Angell" title="James B. Angell" class="mw-redirect">James B. Angell</a> Scholars and are invited to attend the annual Honors Convocation, an event which recognizes undergraduate students with distinguished academic achievements.<sup id="cite_ref-Honors_78-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Honors-78"><span>[</span>79<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Out-of-state undergraduate students pay between <a href="/wiki/United_States_dollar" title="United States dollar">US $</a>36,001.38 and $43,063.38 annually for tuition alone while in-state undergraduate students paid between US $11,837.38 and $16,363.38 annually.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79"><span>[</span>80<span>]</span></a></sup> U-M provides financial aid in the form of need-based loans, grants, scholarships, work study, and non-need based scholarships, with 77% of undergraduates in 2007 receiving financial aid.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80"><span>[</span>81<span>]</span></a></sup> 8% were eligible for <a href="/wiki/Pell_Grant" title="Pell Grant">Pell Grants</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Student_debt_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Student_debt-81"><span>[</span>82<span>]</span></a></sup> For undergraduates in 2008, 46% graduated with about $25,586 of debt in 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-Student_debt_81-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Student_debt-81"><span>[</span>82<span>]</span></a></sup> The university is attempting to increase financial aid availability to students by devoting over $1.53 billion in endowment funds to support financial aid.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82"><span>[</span>83<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83"><span>[</span>84<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84"><span>[</span>85<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Michigan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Student body">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Student_body">Student body</span></h3>
<p>In fall 2010, the university had an enrollment of 41,924 students: 27,027 <a href="/wiki/Undergraduate_education" title="Undergraduate education">undergraduate students</a>, 12,188 academic degree-seeking <a href="/wiki/Graduate_student" title="Graduate student" class="mw-redirect">graduate students</a>, and 2,709 <a href="/wiki/First_professional_degree" title="First professional degree">first professional</a> students.<sup id="cite_ref-enrollment_1-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-enrollment-1"><span>[</span>2<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-size_52-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-size-52"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup> in 600 academic programs. Of all students, 36,650 (87.4 percent) are <a href="/wiki/U.S._citizen" title="U.S. citizen" class="mw-redirect">U.S. citizens</a> or <a href="/wiki/Permanent_residence_(United_States)" title="Permanent residence (United States)">permanent residents</a> and 5,274 (12.6 percent) are <a href="/wiki/International_student" title="International student">international students</a>. Each year, some 30,000 people apply for admission to the university; almost 42 percent are admitted and some 5,400 new students enroll.<sup id="cite_ref-freshman_profiles_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-freshman_profiles-85"><span>[</span>86<span>]</span></a></sup> Students come from all 50 <a href="/wiki/U.S._state" title="U.S. state">U.S. states</a> and more than 100 countries.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86"><span>[</span>87<span>]</span></a></sup> Some 97 percent of the university's incoming class of 2009 earned a <a href="/wiki/High_school" title="High school">high school</a> GPA of 3.0 and higher. The average GPA of accepted applicants in the 2011 class was 3.8. The middle 50 percent of admitted applicants reported an <a href="/wiki/SAT" title="SAT">SAT</a> score of about 1990-2200 (Critical Reading 630-730, Math 670-770, Writing 650-750) and an <a href="/wiki/ACT_(examination)" title="ACT (examination)" class="mw-redirect">ACT</a> score of 29-33, with <a href="/wiki/Advanced_Placement_Program" title="Advanced Placement Program" class="mw-redirect">AP</a> credit granted to over 3000 freshmen students.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87"><span>[</span>88<span>]</span></a></sup> Full-time students make up about 96 percent of the student body. Among full-time students, the university has a first-time student retention rate of 96 percent.<sup id="cite_ref-student_statistics_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-student_statistics-88"><span>[</span>89<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
