<p>In 2010, undergraduates were enrolled in 12 schools: About 62 percent in the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Michigan_College_of_Literature,_Science,_and_the_Arts" title="University of Michigan College of Literature, Science, and the Arts">College of Literature, Science, and the Arts</a>; 21 percent in the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Michigan_College_of_Engineering" title="University of Michigan College of Engineering">College of Engineering</a>; 4 percent in the <a href="/wiki/Ross_School_of_Business" title="Ross School of Business">Ross School of Business</a>; 3 percent in the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Michigan_School_of_Kinesiology" title="University of Michigan School of Kinesiology">School of Kinesiology</a>; 3 percent in the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Michigan_School_of_Music,_Theatre_%26_Dance" title="University of Michigan School of Music, Theatre &amp; Dance">School of Music, Theatre &amp; Dance</a>; and 2 percent in the School of Nursing. Small numbers of undergraduates were enrolled in the colleges or schools of Art &amp; Design, Architecture &amp; Urban Planning, Dentistry, Education, Pharmacy, and Public Policy.<sup id="cite_ref-size_52-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-size-52"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup> Among undergraduates, 70 percent graduate with a bachelor's degree within four years, 86 percent graduate within five years and 88 percent graduating within six years.<sup id="cite_ref-student_statistics_88-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-student_statistics-88"><span>[</span>89<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Of the university's 12,188 non-professional graduate students, 5,367 are seeking <a href="/wiki/Doctorate" title="Doctorate">academic doctorates</a> and 6,821 are seeking <a href="/wiki/Master%27s_degree" title="Master's degree">master's degrees</a>. The largest number of master's degree students are enrolled in the <a href="/wiki/Ross_School_of_Business" title="Ross School of Business">Ross School of Business</a> (1,812 students seeking <a href="/wiki/Master_of_Business_Administration" title="Master of Business Administration">MBA</a> or <a href="/wiki/Master_of_Accounting" title="Master of Accounting" class="mw-redirect">Master of Accounting</a> degrees) and the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Michigan_College_of_Engineering" title="University of Michigan College of Engineering">College of Engineering</a> (1,456 students seeking <a href="/wiki/Master_of_Science" title="Master of Science">M.S.</a> or <a href="/wiki/Master_of_Engineering" title="Master of Engineering">M.Eng.</a> degrees). The largest number of doctoral students are enrolled in the College of Literature, Science, and the Arts (2,076) and College of Engineering (1,496). While the majority of U-M's graduate degree-granting schools and colleges have both undergraduate and graduate students, a few schools only issue graduate degrees. Presently, the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Michigan_School_of_Information" title="University of Michigan School of Information">School of Information</a>, <a href="/wiki/University_of_Michigan_School_of_Natural_Resources_and_Environment" title="University of Michigan School of Natural Resources and Environment">School of Natural Resources and Environment</a>, <a href="/wiki/University_of_Michigan_School_of_Public_Health" title="University of Michigan School of Public Health">School of Public Health</a>, and School of Social Work only have graduate students.<sup id="cite_ref-size_52-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-size-52"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In fall 2010, 2,709 Michigan students were enrolled in U-M's <a href="/wiki/Professional_school" title="Professional school">professional schools</a>: the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Michigan_School_of_Dentistry" title="University of Michigan School of Dentistry">School of Dentistry</a> (439 students), <a href="/wiki/University_of_Michigan_Law_School" title="University of Michigan Law School">Law School</a> (1,182 students), <a href="/wiki/University_of_Michigan_Medical_School" title="University of Michigan Medical School" class="mw-redirect">Medical School</a> (802 students), and <a href="/wiki/University_of_Michigan_College_of_Pharmacy" title="University of Michigan College of Pharmacy">College of Pharmacy</a> (439 students).<sup id="cite_ref-size_52-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-size-52"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Michigan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Research">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Research">Research</span></h3>
<div class="rellink boilerplate seealso">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_University_of_Michigan_faculty_and_staff" title="List of University of Michigan faculty and staff">List of University of Michigan faculty and staff</a></div>
<p>The university is one of the founding members (1900) of the <a href="/wiki/Association_of_American_Universities" title="Association of American Universities">Association of American Universities</a>. With over 6,200 faculty members, 73 of whom are members of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_National_Academies" title="United States National Academies">National Academy</a> and 451 of whom hold an endowed chair in their discipline,<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91"><span>[</span>92<span>]</span></a></sup> the university manages one of the largest annual collegiate research budgets of any university in the United States, totaling about $1 billion in 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-FY09_Expenditures_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FY09_Expenditures-92"><span>[</span>93<span>]</span></a></sup> The Medical School spent the most at over US $445 million, while the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Michigan_College_of_Engineering" title="University of Michigan College of Engineering">College of Engineering</a> was second at more than $160 million.<sup id="cite_ref-FY09_Expenditures_92-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FY09_Expenditures-92"><span>[</span>93<span>]</span></a></sup> U-M also has a <a href="/wiki/Technology_transfer" title="Technology transfer">technology transfer</a> office, which is the university conduit between laboratory research and corporate commercialization interests. In 2009, the university consummated a deal to purchase a facility formerly owned by <a href="/wiki/Pfizer" title="Pfizer">Pfizer</a>. The acquisition includes over 170 acres (0.69&#160;km<sup>2</sup>) of property, and 30 major buildings comprising roughly 1,600,000 feet (490,000 m<sup>2</sup>) of wet laboratory space, and 400,000 square feet (37,000 m<sup>2</sup>) of administrative space. As of the purchase date, the university's intentions for the space were not announced, but the expectation is that the new space will allow the university to ramp up its research and ultimately employ in excess of 2,000 people.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93"><span>[</span>94<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Biomedical_Science_Research_2010.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Building with curving bluish-green glass facade behind a road intersection" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Biomedical_Science_Research_2010.jpg/220px-Biomedical_Science_Research_2010.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Biomedical_Science_Research_2010.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.18/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
A. Alfred Taubman Biomedical Science Research Building at the U-M Medical School</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The university is also a major contributor to the medical field with the <a href="/wiki/Electrocardiogram" title="Electrocardiogram" class="mw-redirect">EKG</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94"><span>[</span>95<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Gastroscope" title="Gastroscope" class="mw-redirect">gastroscope</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95"><span>[</span>96<span>]</span></a></sup> and the announcement of <a href="/wiki/Jonas_Salk" title="Jonas Salk">Jonas Salk</a>'s polio vaccine. The university's 13,000-acre (53&#160;km<sup>2</sup>) <a href="/wiki/University_of_Michigan_Biological_Station" title="University of Michigan Biological Station">biological station</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Michigan" title="Northern Michigan">Northern Lower Peninsula of Michigan</a> is one of only 47 <a href="/wiki/Biosphere_reserve" title="Biosphere reserve" class="mw-redirect">Biosphere Reserves</a> in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96"><span>[</span>97<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In the mid-1960s U-M researchers worked with <a href="/wiki/IBM" title="IBM">IBM</a> to develop a new <a href="/wiki/Virtual_memory" title="Virtual memory">virtual memory</a> architectural model<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97"><span>[</span>98<span>]</span></a></sup> that became part of <a href="/wiki/IBM_System/360_Model_67" title="IBM System/360 Model 67">IBM's Model 360/67</a> <a href="/wiki/Mainframe_computer" title="Mainframe computer">mainframe computer</a> (the 360/67 was initially dubbed the 360/65M where the "M" stood for Michigan).<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98"><span>[</span>99<span>]</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Michigan_Terminal_System" title="Michigan Terminal System">Michigan Terminal System</a> (<a href="/wiki/Michigan_Terminal_System" title="Michigan Terminal System">MTS</a>), an early <a href="/wiki/Time-sharing" title="Time-sharing">time-sharing</a> computer <a href="/wiki/Operating_system" title="Operating system">operating system</a> developed at U-M, was the first system outside of IBM to use the 360/67's virtual memory features.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99"><span>[</span>100<span>]</span></a></sup> In the late 1960s U-M, together with <a href="/wiki/Michigan_State_University" title="Michigan State University">Michigan State University</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wayne_State_University" title="Wayne State University">Wayne State University</a>, founded the <a href="/wiki/Merit_Network" title="Merit Network">Merit Network</a>, one of the first university computer networks.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100"><span>[</span>101<span>]</span></a></sup> The Merit Network was then and remains today administratively hosted by U-M. Another major contribution took place in 1987 when a proposal submitted by the Merit Network together with its partners <a href="/wiki/IBM" title="IBM">IBM</a>, <a href="/wiki/MCI_Communications" title="MCI Communications">MCI</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/State_of_Michigan" title="State of Michigan" class="mw-redirect">State of Michigan</a> won a national competition to upgrade and expand the <a href="/wiki/NSFNET" title="NSFNET" class="mw-redirect">National Science Foundation Network</a> (<a href="/wiki/NSFNET" title="NSFNET" class="mw-redirect">NSFNET</a>) backbone from 56,000 to 1.5 million, and later to 45 million bits per second.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101"><span>[</span>102<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/NSFNET" title="NSFNET" class="mw-redirect">NSFNET</a> was the precursor to today's <a href="/wiki/Internet" title="Internet">Internet</a>.</p>
<p>In 2006, U-M joined with <a href="/wiki/Michigan_State_University" title="Michigan State University">Michigan State University</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wayne_State_University" title="Wayne State University">Wayne State University</a> to create the <a href="/wiki/University_Research_Corridor" title="University Research Corridor">University Research Corridor</a>. This effort was undertaken to highlight the capabilities of the state's three leading research institutions and drive the transformation of Michigan's economy.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102"><span>[</span>103<span>]</span></a></sup> The 3 universities are electronically connected via Michigan LambdaRail (MiLR, pronounced 'MY-lar').<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103"><span>[</span>104<span>]</span></a></sup> Initially MiLR enabled researchers at MSU, U-M and WSU to transfer data at 10 billion bits per second or 1,000 to 10,000 times faster than the Internet connections normally used in homes and businesses today. The speed and reliability of the new network will enable doctors to perform virtual surgery at remote locations. The network provides the capacity for physicists to share exceptionally large data sets with their colleagues around the world. The new network also will serve as a test-bed for experimental research on networking itself. MiLR, which employs advanced optical electronics, will use more than 750 miles (1,210&#160;km) of fiber-optic cabling, most of it already in place, to connect the universities to each other and to national and international networking hubs in Chicago. Those hubs include the National LambdaRail, StarLight, and an emerging set of network connections that play key roles in the national cyberinfrastructure supporting advanced science and research.</p>
<p>U-M is home to the <a href="/wiki/National_Election_Studies" title="National Election Studies" class="mw-redirect">National Election Studies</a> and the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Michigan_Consumer_Sentiment_Index" title="University of Michigan Consumer Sentiment Index">University of Michigan Consumer Sentiment Index</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Correlates_of_War" title="Correlates of War">Correlates of War</a> project, also located at U-M, is an accumulation of scientific knowledge about war. The university is also home to major research centers in <a href="/wiki/Optics" title="Optics">optics</a>, reconfigurable manufacturing systems, <a href="/wiki/Wireless_integrated_microsystems" title="Wireless integrated microsystems" class="mw-redirect">wireless integrated microsystems</a>, and social sciences. The University of Michigan Transportation Research Institute and the Life Sciences Institute are located at the university. The Institute for Social Research (ISR), the nation's longest-standing laboratory for interdisciplinary research in the social sciences,<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104"><span>[</span>105<span>]</span></a></sup> is home to the Survey Research Center, Research Center for Group Dynamics, Center for Political Studies, Population Studies Center, and Inter-Consortium for Political and Social Research. Undergraduate students are able to participate in various research projects through the <a href="/wiki/Undergraduate_Research_Opportunity_Program" title="Undergraduate Research Opportunity Program" class="mw-redirect">Undergraduate Research Opportunity Program</a> (UROP) as well as the UROP/Creative-Programs.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105"><span>[</span>106<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/University_of_Michigan_Library" title="University of Michigan Library">U-M library system</a> comprises nineteen individual libraries with twenty-four separate collections—roughly 9.6 million volumes, growing at the rate of 177,000 volumes a year.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106"><span>[</span>107<span>]</span></a></sup> U-M was the original home of the <a href="/wiki/JSTOR" title="JSTOR">JSTOR</a> database, which contains about 750,000 digitized pages from the entire pre-1990 backfile of ten journals of history and economics, and has initiated a book digitization program in collaboration with <a href="/wiki/Google" title="Google">Google</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107"><span>[</span>108<span>]</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/University_of_Michigan_Press" title="University of Michigan Press">University of Michigan Press</a> is also a part of the U-M library system.</p>
<h2><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Michigan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Student life">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Student_life">Student life</span></h2>
<h3><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Michigan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Residential life">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Residential_life">Residential life</span></h3>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/University_of_Michigan_Housing" title="University of Michigan Housing" class="mw-redirect">University of Michigan Housing</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:UMichNorthQuad2011.JPG" class="image"><img alt="Red brick facade with white stone fronts and angled roof" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/UMichNorthQuad2011.JPG/220px-UMichNorthQuad2011.JPG" width="220" height="331" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:UMichNorthQuad2011.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.18/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
North Quad Residence Hall</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The University of Michigan's campus housing system can accommodate up to 10,900 people, or nearly 30 percent of the total student population at the university.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108"><span>[</span>109<span>]</span></a></sup> The residence halls are organized into three distinct groups: Central Campus, Hill Area (between Central Campus and the University of Michigan Medical Center) and North Campus. Family housing is located on North Campus and mainly serves graduate students. The largest residence hall has a capacity of 1,240 students,<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109"><span>[</span>110<span>]</span></a></sup> while the smallest accommodates 25 residents.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110"><span>[</span>111<span>]</span></a></sup> A majority of upper-division and graduate students live in off-campus apartments, houses, and <a href="/wiki/Housing_cooperative" title="Housing cooperative">cooperatives</a>, with the largest concentrations in the Central and South Campus areas.</p>
<p>The residential system has a number of "living-learning communities" where academic activities and residential life are combined. These communities focus on areas such as research through the Michigan Research Community, medical sciences, <a href="/wiki/Community_service" title="Community service">community service</a> and the <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German language</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111"><span>[</span>112<span>]</span></a></sup> The Michigan Research Community and the Women in Science and Engineering Residence Program are housed in Mosher-Jordan Hall. The Residential College (RC), a living-learning community that is a division of the College of Literature, Science and the Arts, also has its principal instructional space in East Quad. The Lloyd Hall Scholars Program(LHSP) and the Health Sciences Scholars Program (HSSP) is located in Alice Lloyd Hall. In fall 2010, North Quad - the first new residential hall to be built on campus since 1967 - opened. This residence complex is composed of two additional living-learning communities: the <a href="/wiki/Global_Scholars_Program,_University_of_Michigan,_Ann_Arbor" title="Global Scholars Program, University of Michigan, Ann Arbor">Global Scholars Program</a><sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112"><span>[</span>113<span>]</span></a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Max_Kade" title="Max Kade">Max Kade German Program</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113"><span>[</span>114<span>]</span></a></sup> It is "technology-rich," and houses communication-related programs, including the School of Information, the Department of Communication Studies, and the Department of Screen Arts and Cultures.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114"><span>[</span>115<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115"><span>[</span>116<span>]</span></a></sup> North Quad is also home to services such as the Language Resource Center and the Sweetland Center for Writing.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116"><span>[</span>117<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Michigan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Groups and activities">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Groups_and_activities">Groups and activities</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Michigan_Union_2009.JPG" class="image"><img alt="Red brick building with large windows, tall central tower, and green ivy growing on the facade" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Michigan_Union_2009.JPG/220px-Michigan_Union_2009.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Michigan_Union_2009.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.18/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
Michigan Union on Central Campus</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The University lists 1,279 student organizations.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117"><span>[</span>118<span>]</span></a></sup> With a history of student activism, some of the most visible groups include those dedicated to causes such as <a href="/wiki/Civil_rights" title="Civil rights" class="mw-redirect">civil rights</a> and <a href="/wiki/Labor_rights" title="Labor rights">labor rights</a>. One group is <a href="/wiki/Students_for_a_Democratic_Society_(2006_organization)" title="Students for a Democratic Society (2006 organization)">Students for a Democratic Society</a>, which recently reformed with a new chapter on campus as of February 2007. Though the student body generally leans toward <a href="/wiki/Left-wing_politics" title="Left-wing politics">left-wing politics</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118"><span>[</span>119<span>]</span></a></sup> there are also conservative groups, such as <a href="/wiki/Young_Americans_for_Freedom" title="Young Americans for Freedom">Young Americans for Freedom</a>, and non-partisan groups, such as the Roosevelt Institution.</p>
<p>There are also several engineering projects teams, including the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Michigan_Solar_Car_Team" title="University of Michigan Solar Car Team">University of Michigan Solar Car Team</a>, which placed first in the <a href="/wiki/North_American_Solar_Challenge" title="North American Solar Challenge">North American Solar Challenge</a> six times and third in the <a href="/wiki/World_Solar_Challenge" title="World Solar Challenge">World Solar Challenge</a> four times.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119"><span>[</span>120<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Michigan_Interactive_Investments" title="Michigan Interactive Investments">Michigan Interactive Investments</a>, the <a href="/wiki/TAMID_Israel_Investment_Group" title="TAMID Israel Investment Group">TAMID Israel Investment Group</a>, and the Michigan Economics Society<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120"><span>[</span>121<span>]</span></a></sup> are also affiliated with the university.</p>
<p>The university also showcases many community service organizations and charitable projects, including <a href="/wiki/Foundation_for_International_Medical_Relief_of_Children" title="Foundation for International Medical Relief of Children">Foundation for International Medical Relief of Children</a>, Dance Marathon at the University of Michigan,<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121"><span>[</span>122<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/The_Detroit_Partnership" title="The Detroit Partnership">The Detroit Partnership</a>, <a href="/wiki/Relay_for_Life" title="Relay for Life" class="mw-redirect">Relay For Life</a>, U-M Stars for the Make-A-Wish Foundation, InnoWorks at the University of Michigan, SERVE, Letters to Success, PROVIDES, Circle K, <a href="/wiki/Habitat_for_Humanity" title="Habitat for Humanity">Habitat for Humanity</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122"><span>[</span>123<span>]</span></a></sup> and Ann Arbor Reaching Out. <a href="/wiki/Intramural_sports" title="Intramural sports">Intramural sports</a> are popular, and there are recreation facilities for each of the three campuses.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123"><span>[</span>124<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Fraternities_and_sororities" title="Fraternities and sororities">Fraternities and sororities</a> play a role in the university's social life; approximately 18 percent of undergraduates are involved in Greek life. Membership numbers for the 2009-2010 school year reached the highest in the last two decades. Four different Greek councils - the Interfraternity Council, Multicultural Greek Council, National Pan-Hellenic Council, and Panhellenic Association - represent most Greek organizations. Each council has a different recruitment process.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124"><span>[</span>125<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Michigan_Union" title="Michigan Union">Michigan Union</a> and Michigan League are student activity centers located on Central Campus; Pierpont Commons is on North Campus. The Michigan Union houses a majority of student groups, including the student government. The William Monroe Trotter House, located east of Central Campus, is a multicultural student center operated by the university's Office of Multi-Ethnic Student Affairs.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125"><span>[</span>126<span>]</span></a></sup> The University Activities Center (UAC) is a student-run programming organization and is composed of 14 committees.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126"><span>[</span>127<span>]</span></a></sup> Each group involves students in the planning and execution of a variety of events both on and off campus.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Michigan_Marching_Band" title="Michigan Marching Band">Michigan Marching Band</a>, composed of more than 350 students from almost all of U-M's schools,<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127"><span>[</span>128<span>]</span></a></sup> is the university's <a href="/wiki/Marching_band" title="Marching band">marching band</a>. Over 100 years old,<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128"><span>[</span>129<span>]</span></a></sup> the band performs at every home football game and travels to at least one away game a year. The student-run and led <a href="/wiki/University_of_Michigan_Pops_Orchestra" title="University of Michigan Pops Orchestra">University of Michigan Pops Orchestra</a> is another musical ensemble that attracts students from all academic backgrounds. It performs regularly in the <a href="/wiki/Michigan_Theater_(Ann_Arbor)" title="Michigan Theater (Ann Arbor)" class="mw-redirect">Michigan Theater</a>. The <a href="/wiki/University_of_Michigan_Men%27s_Glee_Club" title="University of Michigan Men's Glee Club">University of Michigan Men's Glee Club</a>, founded in 1859 and the second oldest such group in the country, is a men's chorus with over 100 members.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129"><span>[</span>130<span>]</span></a></sup> Its eight member subset <a href="/wiki/A_cappella" title="A cappella">a cappella</a> group, the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Michigan_Friars" title="University of Michigan Friars" class="mw-redirect">University of Michigan Friars</a>, which was founded in 1955, is the oldest currently running <i>a cappella</i> group on campus.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130"><span>[</span>131<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Student_newspaper" title="Student newspaper">student newspaper</a> is <i><a href="/wiki/The_Michigan_Daily" title="The Michigan Daily">The Michigan Daily</a></i>, founded in 1890 and editorially and financially independent of the university. <i>The Daily</i> is published five days a week during academic year, and weekly from May to August. Other student publications at the university include the conservative <i><a href="/wiki/The_Michigan_Review" title="The Michigan Review">The Michigan Review</a></i> and the progressive <i>Michigan Independent</i>. The humor publications <i><a href="/wiki/The_Michigan_Every_Three_Weekly" title="The Michigan Every Three Weekly">The Michigan Every Three Weekly</a></i> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Gargoyle_Humor_Magazine" title="Gargoyle Humor Magazine">Gargoyle</a></i> are also published by Michigan students.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/WCBN-FM" title="WCBN-FM">WCBN-FM</a> (88.3 FM) is the student-run <a href="/wiki/College_radio" title="College radio" class="mw-redirect">college radio</a> station which plays in <a href="/wiki/Freeform_(radio_format)" title="Freeform (radio format)">freeform format</a>. <a href="/wiki/WOLV_(TV)" title="WOLV (TV)">WOLV-TV</a> is the student-run television station that is primarily shown on the university's cable television system.</p>
<p>Several <a href="/wiki/Academic_journal" title="Academic journal">academic journals</a> are published at the university. The Ross School of Business publishes the <i><a href="/wiki/Michigan_Journal_of_Business" title="Michigan Journal of Business">Michigan Journal of Business</a></i>. The Law School publishes the well-regarded <i><a href="/wiki/Michigan_Law_Review" title="Michigan Law Review">Michigan Law Review</a></i> as well as the <i><a href="/wiki/University_of_Michigan_Journal_of_Law_Reform" title="University of Michigan Journal of Law Reform">University of Michigan Journal of Law Reform</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Michigan_Journal_of_Race_%26_Law" title="Michigan Journal of Race &amp; Law">Michigan Journal of Race &amp; Law</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Michigan_Telecommunications_and_Technology_Law_Review" title="Michigan Telecommunications and Technology Law Review">Michigan Telecommunications and Technology Law Review</a></i>, <i>Michigan Journal of International Law</i>, and <i>Michigan Journal of Gender &amp; Law</i>. Several undergraduate journals are also published at the university, including the <i><a href="/wiki/Michigan_Journal_of_Political_Science" title="Michigan Journal of Political Science">Michigan Journal of Political Science</a></i>, <i>Michigan Journal of History</i>, <i>University of Michigan Undergraduate Research Journal,</i> and "<a href="/w/index.php?title=Michigan_Journal_of_International_Affairs&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Michigan Journal of International Affairs (page does not exist)">Michigan Journal of International Affairs</a>."</p>
<h2><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Michigan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Athletics">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Athletics">Athletics</span></h2>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Michigan_Wolverines" title="Michigan Wolverines">Michigan Wolverines</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:MichiganStadium2010UConn.JPG" class="image"><img alt="Crowded stadium with yellow-colored &quot;Michigan&quot; written on a green field" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/MichiganStadium2010UConn.JPG/220px-MichiganStadium2010UConn.JPG" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:MichiganStadium2010UConn.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.18/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
A football game at <a href="/wiki/Michigan_Stadium" title="Michigan Stadium">Michigan Stadium</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The University of Michigan's sports teams are called the <a href="/wiki/Michigan_Wolverines" title="Michigan Wolverines">Wolverines</a>. They participate in the <a href="/wiki/National_Collegiate_Athletic_Association" title="National Collegiate Athletic Association">NCAA's</a> Football Bowl Subdivision (formerly <a href="/wiki/Division_I-A" title="Division I-A" class="mw-redirect">Division I-A</a>) and in the <a href="/wiki/Big_Ten_Conference" title="Big Ten Conference">Big Ten Conference</a> in all sports except men's <a href="/wiki/Ice_hockey" title="Ice hockey">ice hockey</a>, which is a member of the <a href="/wiki/Central_Collegiate_Hockey_Association" title="Central Collegiate Hockey Association">Central Collegiate Hockey Association</a>, and woman's water polo, which is a member of the Collegiate Water Polo Association. In 10 of the past 14 years concluding in 2009, U-M has finished in the top five of the <a href="/wiki/NACDA_Director%27s_Cup" title="NACDA Director's Cup" class="mw-redirect">NACDA Director's Cup</a>, a ranking compiled by the <a href="/wiki/National_Association_of_Collegiate_Directors_of_Athletics" title="National Association of Collegiate Directors of Athletics">National Association of Collegiate Directors of Athletics</a> to tabulate the success of universities in competitive sports. U-M has finished in the top ten of the Directors' Cup standings in 14 of the award's sixteen seasons and has placed in the top six in 9 of the last 10 seasons.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131"><span>[</span>132<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Michigan_Wolverines_football" title="Michigan Wolverines football">Michigan football</a> program ranks first in NCAA history in both total wins (884 through the end of the 2010 season) and winning percentage (.735).<sup id="cite_ref-football_records_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-football_records-18"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup> The team won the first <a href="/wiki/Rose_Bowl_Game" title="Rose Bowl Game">Rose Bowl</a> game in 1902. U-M had 40 consecutive winning seasons from 1968 to 2007, including consecutive <a href="/wiki/Bowl_game" title="Bowl game">bowl game</a> appearances from 1975 to 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132"><span>[</span>133<span>]</span></a></sup> The Wolverines have won a record 42 Big Ten championships. The program has eleven <a href="/wiki/College_football_national_championships_in_NCAA_Division_I_FBS" title="College football national championships in NCAA Division I FBS">national championships</a>, most recently in 1997,<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133"><span>[</span>134<span>]</span></a></sup> and has produced three <a href="/wiki/Heisman_Trophy" title="Heisman Trophy">Heisman Trophy</a> winners: <a href="/wiki/Tom_Harmon" title="Tom Harmon">Tom Harmon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Desmond_Howard" title="Desmond Howard">Desmond Howard</a> and <a href="/wiki/Charles_Woodson" title="Charles Woodson">Charles Woodson</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Heisman_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Heisman-134"><span>[</span>135<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Michigan_Stadium" title="Michigan Stadium">Michigan Stadium</a> is the largest college football stadium in the nation and one of the largest football-only stadiums in the world, with an official capacity of more than 109,901<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135"><span>[</span>136<span>]</span></a></sup> (the extra seat is said to be "reserved" for <a href="/wiki/Fritz_Crisler" title="Fritz Crisler">Fritz Crisler</a><sup id="cite_ref-Michigan_Stadium_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Michigan_Stadium-136"><span>[</span>137<span>]</span></a></sup>) though attendance—frequently over 111,000 spectators—regularly exceeds the official capacity.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137"><span>[</span>138<span>]</span></a></sup> The NCAA's record-breaking attendance has become commonplace at Michigan Stadium, especially since the arrival of head coach <a href="/wiki/Bo_Schembechler" title="Bo Schembechler">Bo Schembechler</a>. U-M has fierce rivalries with many teams, including <a href="/wiki/Michigan_State_University" title="Michigan State University">Michigan State</a>, <a href="/wiki/University_of_Notre_Dame" title="University of Notre Dame">Notre Dame</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ohio_State_University" title="Ohio State University">Ohio State</a>, the last of which has been referred to by <a href="/wiki/ESPN" title="ESPN">ESPN</a> as the <a href="/wiki/Michigan-Ohio_State_Rivalry" title="Michigan-Ohio State Rivalry" class="mw-redirect">greatest rivalry</a> in American sports.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138"><span>[</span>139<span>]</span></a></sup> U-M has all-time winning records against Michigan State University, University of Notre Dame, and Ohio State University.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139"><span>[</span>140<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ray_Fisher_baseball_stadium_Ann_Arbor_Michigan.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Ray_Fisher_baseball_stadium_Ann_Arbor_Michigan.JPG/220px-Ray_Fisher_baseball_stadium_Ann_Arbor_Michigan.JPG" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ray_Fisher_baseball_stadium_Ann_Arbor_Michigan.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.18/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Ray_Fisher" title="Ray Fisher">Ray Fisher</a> <a href="/wiki/Ray_Fisher_Stadium" title="Ray Fisher Stadium">baseball stadium</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The men's <a href="/wiki/Michigan_Wolverines_men%27s_ice_hockey" title="Michigan Wolverines men's ice hockey">ice hockey</a> team, which plays at <a href="/wiki/Yost_Ice_Arena" title="Yost Ice Arena">Yost Ice Arena</a>, has won nine <a href="/wiki/NCAA_Men%27s_Ice_Hockey_Championship" title="NCAA Men's Ice Hockey Championship">national championships</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140"><span>[</span>141<span>]</span></a></sup> while the <a href="/wiki/Michigan_Wolverines_men%27s_basketball" title="Michigan Wolverines men's basketball">men's basketball</a> team, which plays at <a href="/wiki/Crisler_Arena" title="Crisler Arena">Crisler Arena</a>, has appeared in four <a href="/wiki/NCAA_Men%27s_Division_I_Basketball_Championship" title="NCAA Men's Division I Basketball Championship">Final Fours</a> and won the <a href="/wiki/NCAA_Men%27s_Division_I_Basketball_Championship" title="NCAA Men's Division I Basketball Championship">national championship</a> in 1989. However, the program became involved in a <a href="/wiki/University_of_Michigan_basketball_scandal" title="University of Michigan basketball scandal">scandal involving payments from a booster</a> during the 1990s. This led to the program being placed on probation for a four-year period. The program also voluntarily vacated victories from its 1992–1993 and 1995–1999 seasons in which the payments took place, as well as its 1992 and 1993 Final Four appearances.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141"><span>[</span>142<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Through the <a href="/wiki/2008_Summer_Olympic_Games" title="2008 Summer Olympic Games" class="mw-redirect">2008 Summer Olympic Games</a>, 178 U-M students and coaches had participated in the Olympics, winning medals in every <a href="/wiki/Summer_Olympic_Games" title="Summer Olympic Games">Summer Olympics</a> except 1896, and winning gold medals in all but four Olympiads. U-M students have won a total of <a href="/wiki/Michigan_Wolverines#Olympians" title="Michigan Wolverines">133 Olympic medals</a>: 65 gold, 30 silver, and 38 bronze. In 2012 the university will field a varsity mens lacrosse team, and a women's team in 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-Olympians_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Olympians-142"><span>[</span>143<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Michigan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: School songs">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="School_songs">School songs</span></h3>
<p>The University of Michigan's <a href="/wiki/Fight_song" title="Fight song">fight song</a>, "<a href="/wiki/The_Victors" title="The Victors">The Victors</a>," was written by student <a href="/wiki/Louis_Elbel" title="Louis Elbel" class="mw-redirect">Louis Elbel</a> in 1898 following the last-minute football victory over the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Chicago" title="University of Chicago">University of Chicago</a> that won a league championship. The song was declared by <a href="/wiki/John_Philip_Sousa" title="John Philip Sousa">John Philip Sousa</a> as "the greatest college fight song ever written."<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143"><span>[</span>144<span>]</span></a></sup> The song refers to the university as being "the Champions of the West." At the time, U-M was part of the Western Conference, which would later become the <a href="/wiki/Big_Ten_Conference" title="Big Ten Conference">Big Ten Conference</a>. Michigan was considered to be on the Western Frontier when it was founded in the old Northwest Territory. Although mainly used at sporting events, the fight song can be heard at other events. President <a href="/wiki/Gerald_Ford" title="Gerald Ford">Gerald Ford</a> had it played by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Marine_Band" title="United States Marine Band">United States Marine Band</a> as his entrance anthem during his term as president from 1974 to 1977, in preference over the more traditional "<a href="/wiki/Hail_to_the_Chief" title="Hail to the Chief">Hail to the Chief</a>"<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144"><span>[</span>145<span>]</span></a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Michigan_Marching_Band" title="Michigan Marching Band">Michigan Marching Band</a> performed a slow-tempo variation on the fight song at his funeral.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145"><span>[</span>146<span>]</span></a></sup> The fight song is also sung during graduation commencement ceremonies. The university's <a href="/wiki/Alma_mater" title="Alma mater">alma mater</a> song is "<a href="/wiki/The_Yellow_and_Blue" title="The Yellow and Blue">The Yellow and Blue</a>." A common rally cry is "Let's Go Blue!," written by former students Joseph Carl, a sousaphonist, and Albert Ahronheim, a <a href="/wiki/Drum_major" title="Drum major">drum major</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146"><span>[</span>147<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Michigan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Notable people and alumni">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Notable_people_and_alumni">Notable people and alumni</span></h2>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_University_of_Michigan_alumni" title="List of University of Michigan alumni">List of University of Michigan alumni</a></div>
<p>In addition to the late <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">U.S. president</a> <a href="/wiki/Gerald_Ford" title="Gerald Ford">Gerald Ford</a>, the university has produced twenty-six <a href="/wiki/Rhodes_Scholarship" title="Rhodes Scholarship">Rhodes Scholars</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Rhodes_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rhodes-147"><span>[</span>148<span>]</span></a></sup> numerous Marshall Scholars, seven <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize" title="Nobel Prize">Nobel Prize</a> winners, 116 <a href="/wiki/Olympic_medalist" title="Olympic medalist" class="mw-redirect">Olympic medalists</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Olympians_142-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Olympians-142"><span>[</span>143<span>]</span></a></sup> 17 <a href="/wiki/MacArthur_Foundation" title="MacArthur Foundation" class="mw-redirect">MacArthur Foundation</a> award winners, 18 <a href="/wiki/Pulitzer_Prize" title="Pulitzer Prize">Pulitzer Prize</a> winners including <a href="/wiki/Ann_Marie_Lipinski" title="Ann Marie Lipinski">Ann Marie Lapinski</a> who was named Pulitzer board co-chair in 2011, and <a href="/wiki/Fields_Medal" title="Fields Medal">Fields Medal</a> winner <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Smale" title="Stephen Smale">Stephen Smale</a>. More than 250 Michigan graduates have served as <a href="/wiki/List_of_University_of_Michigan_legislator_alumni" title="List of University of Michigan legislator alumni">legislators</a> as either United States Senator (40 graduates) or as a Congressional representative (over 200 graduates). In the late 1990s and early 2000s, the university routinely has led in the number of <a href="/wiki/Fulbright_Program" title="Fulbright Program">Fulbright Scholars</a> including a nation leading 40 scholars in the 2010/2011 academic year.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148"><span>[</span>149<span>]</span></a></sup> U-M's contributions to aeronautics include aircraft designer <a href="/wiki/Clarence_%22Kelly%22_Johnson" title="Clarence &quot;Kelly&quot; Johnson" class="mw-redirect">Clarence "Kelly" Johnson</a> of <a href="/wiki/Lockheed_Corporation" title="Lockheed Corporation">Lockheed</a> <a href="/wiki/Skunk_Works" title="Skunk Works">Skunk Works</a> fame,<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149"><span>[</span>150<span>]</span></a></sup> Lockheed president <a href="/wiki/Willis_Hawkins" title="Willis Hawkins">Willis Hawkins</a>, and several astronauts including the all-U-M crew of <a href="/wiki/Gemini_4" title="Gemini 4">Gemini 4</a><sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150"><span>[</span>151<span>]</span></a></sup> and the all-Michigan crew of <a href="/wiki/Apollo_15" title="Apollo 15">Apollo 15</a>. U-M counts among its matriculants <a href="/wiki/List_of_University_of_Michigan_business_alumni#Billionaires" title="List of University of Michigan business alumni">nineteen billionaires</a> and prominent company founders and co-founders including <a href="/wiki/Google" title="Google">Google</a> co-founder <a href="/wiki/Larry_Page" title="Larry Page">Larry Page</a><sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151"><span>[</span>152<span>]</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/John_Robert_Beyster" title="John Robert Beyster">Dr. J. Robert Beyster</a> who founded <a href="/wiki/Science_Applications_International_Corporation" title="Science Applications International Corporation" class="mw-redirect">Science Applications International Corporation</a> (SAIC) in 1969.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152"><span>[</span>153<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Notable writers who attended U-M include playwright <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Miller" title="Arthur Miller">Arthur Miller</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Famous_UM_alumni_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Famous_UM_alumni-153"><span>[</span>154<span>]</span></a></sup> essayists <a href="/wiki/Susan_Orlean" title="Susan Orlean">Susan Orlean</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sven_Birkerts" title="Sven Birkerts">Sven Birkerts</a>, journalists and editors <a href="/wiki/Mike_Wallace_(journalist)" title="Mike Wallace (journalist)">Mike Wallace</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Famous_UM_alumni_153-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Famous_UM_alumni-153"><span>[</span>154<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jonathan_Chait" title="Jonathan Chait">Jonathan Chait</a> of <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_Republic" title="The New Republic">The New Republic</a></i>, and <a href="/wiki/Sandra_Steingraber" title="Sandra Steingraber">Sandra Steingraber</a>, food critics <a href="/wiki/Ruth_Reichl" title="Ruth Reichl">Ruth Reichl</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gael_Greene" title="Gael Greene">Gael Greene</a>, novelists <a href="/wiki/Brett_Ellen_Block" title="Brett Ellen Block">Brett Ellen Block</a>, <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_Kostova" title="Elizabeth Kostova">Elizabeth Kostova</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marge_Piercy" title="Marge Piercy">Marge Piercy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brad_Meltzer" title="Brad Meltzer">Brad Meltzer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Betty_Smith" title="Betty Smith">Betty Smith</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Famous_UM_alumni_153-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Famous_UM_alumni-153"><span>[</span>154<span>]</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Charles_Major" title="Charles Major">Charles Major</a>, screenwriter <a href="/wiki/Judith_Guest" title="Judith Guest">Judith Guest</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Famous_UM_alumni_153-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Famous_UM_alumni-153"><span>[</span>154<span>]</span></a></sup> Pulitzer Prize-winning poet <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Roethke" title="Theodore Roethke">Theodore Roethke</a>, National Book Award winner <a href="/wiki/Keith_Waldrop" title="Keith Waldrop">Keith Waldrop</a> and composer/author/puppeteer <a href="/wiki/Forman_Brown" title="Forman Brown">Forman Brown</a>.</p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Cinema_of_the_United_States" title="Cinema of the United States">Hollywood</a>, famous alumni include actors <a href="/wiki/James_Earl_Jones" title="James Earl Jones">James Earl Jones</a>, <a href="/wiki/David_Alan_Grier" title="David Alan Grier">David Alan Grier</a><sup id="cite_ref-Famous_UM_alumni_153-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Famous_UM_alumni-153"><span>[</span>154<span>]</span></a></sup> actresses <a href="/wiki/Lucy_Liu" title="Lucy Liu">Lucy Liu</a> and <a href="/wiki/Selma_Blair" title="Selma Blair">Selma Blair</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Famous_UM_alumni_153-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Famous_UM_alumni-153"><span>[</span>154<span>]</span></a></sup> and filmmaker <a href="/wiki/Lawrence_Kasdan" title="Lawrence Kasdan">Lawrence Kasdan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Famous_UM_alumni_153-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Famous_UM_alumni-153"><span>[</span>154<span>]</span></a></sup> Many Broadway and musical theatre actors, including <a href="/wiki/Gavin_Creel" title="Gavin Creel">Gavin Creel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chelsea_Krombach" title="Chelsea Krombach">Chelsea Krombach</a>, <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Keenan-Bolger" title="Andrew Keenan-Bolger">Andrew Keenan-Bolger</a>, and his sister <a href="/wiki/Celia_Keenan-Bolger" title="Celia Keenan-Bolger">Celia Keenan-Bolger</a> attended U-M for musical theatre. The creators of <i><a href="/wiki/A_Very_Potter_Musical" title="A Very Potter Musical">A Very Potter Musical</a></i>, known as <a href="/wiki/Team_StarKid" title="Team StarKid" class="mw-redirect">Team StarKid</a>, also graduated from the University of Michigan. A member of Team Starkid, actor and singer <a href="/wiki/Darren_Criss" title="Darren Criss">Darren Criss</a>, is a series regular on the television series <a href="/wiki/Glee_(TV_series)" title="Glee (TV series)"><i>Glee</i></a>.</p>
<p>Musical graduates include operatic soprano <a href="/wiki/Jessye_Norman" title="Jessye Norman">Jessye Norman</a>, singer <a href="/wiki/Joe_Dassin" title="Joe Dassin">Joe Dassin</a>, jazz guitarist <a href="/wiki/Randy_Napoleon" title="Randy Napoleon">Randy Napoleon</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mannheim_Steamroller" title="Mannheim Steamroller">Mannheim Steamroller</a> founder <a href="/wiki/Chip_Davis" title="Chip Davis">Chip Davis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Famous_UM_alumni_153-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Famous_UM_alumni-153"><span>[</span>154<span>]</span></a></sup> Classical composer <a href="/wiki/Frank_Ticheli" title="Frank Ticheli">Frank Ticheli</a> and Broadway composer <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Lippa" title="Andrew Lippa">Andrew Lippa</a> attended. Pop Superstar <a href="/wiki/Madonna_(entertainer)" title="Madonna (entertainer)">Madonna</a> and rock legend <a href="/wiki/Iggy_Pop" title="Iggy Pop">Iggy Pop</a> attended but did not graduate.</p>
<p>Other U-M graduates include <a href="/wiki/Claude_Shannon" title="Claude Shannon">Claude Shannon</a> who made major contributions to the mathematics of <a href="/wiki/Information_theory" title="Information theory">information theory</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154"><span>[</span>155<span>]</span></a></sup> current Vice Chairman of the Board of Governors of the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Reserve_System" title="Federal Reserve System">Federal Reserve System</a> <a href="/wiki/Donald_Kohn" title="Donald Kohn">Donald Kohn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Temel_Kotil" title="Temel Kotil">Temel Kotil</a> who is the president and CEO of <a href="/wiki/Turkish_Airlines" title="Turkish Airlines">Turkish Airlines</a>, current Dean of <a href="/wiki/Harvard_Law_School" title="Harvard Law School">Harvard Law School</a> <a href="/wiki/Martha_Minow" title="Martha Minow">Martha Minow</a>, former House Minority Leader <a href="/wiki/Dick_Gephardt" title="Dick Gephardt">Dick Gephardt</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Famous_UM_alumni_153-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Famous_UM_alumni-153"><span>[</span>154<span>]</span></a></sup> US Representative <a href="/wiki/Justin_Amash" title="Justin Amash">Justin Amash</a>, who represents <a href="/wiki/Michigan%27s_3rd_congressional_district" title="Michigan's 3rd congressional district">Michigan's Third Congressional District</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155"><span>[</span>156<span>]</span></a></sup> assisted-suicide advocate Dr. <a href="/wiki/Jack_Kevorkian" title="Jack Kevorkian">Jack Kevorkian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Weather_Underground" title="Weather Underground">Weather Underground</a> radical activist <a href="/wiki/Bill_Ayers" title="Bill Ayers">Bill Ayers</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156"><span>[</span>157<span>]</span></a></sup> activist <a href="/wiki/Tom_Hayden" title="Tom Hayden">Tom Hayden</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Famous_UM_alumni_153-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Famous_UM_alumni-153"><span>[</span>154<span>]</span></a></sup> architect <a href="/wiki/Charles_Willard_Moore" title="Charles Willard Moore">Charles Moore</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157"><span>[</span>158<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ryan_Drummond" title="Ryan Drummond">Ryan Drummond</a> who was the voice of <a href="/wiki/Sonic_the_Hedgehog_(character)" title="Sonic the Hedgehog (character)">Sonic the Hedgehog</a> in the series of video games from 1999–2004, the Swedish Holocaust hero <a href="/wiki/Raoul_Wallenberg" title="Raoul Wallenberg">Raoul Wallenberg</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158"><span>[</span>159<span>]</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_D._Pritchard" title="Benjamin D. Pritchard">Benjamin D. Pritchard</a>, the Civil War general who captured <a href="/wiki/Jefferson_Davis" title="Jefferson Davis">Jefferson Davis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159"><span>[</span>160<span>]</span></a></sup> Neurosurgeon and CNN chief medical correspondent <a href="/wiki/Sanjay_Gupta" title="Sanjay Gupta">Sanjay Gupta</a> attended both college and medical school at U-M.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160"><span>[</span>161<span>]</span></a></sup> Conservative pundit <a href="/wiki/Ann_Coulter" title="Ann Coulter">Ann Coulter</a> attended law school at U-M, as did <a href="/wiki/Clarence_Darrow" title="Clarence Darrow">Clarence Darrow</a> at a time when many lawyers did not receive any formal education. <a href="/wiki/Vaughn_R._Walker" title="Vaughn R. Walker" class="mw-redirect">Vaughn R. Walker</a>, who is a Federal District Judge in California and overturned the controversial <a href="/wiki/California_Proposition_8_(2008)" title="California Proposition 8 (2008)" class="mw-redirect">California Proposition 8</a> in 2010 and ruled it unconstitutional, received his undergraduate degree from U-M in 1966.</p>
<p>Some more notorious graduates of the University are 1910 convicted murderer (though perhaps wrongfully so)<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161"><span>[</span>162<span>]</span></a></sup> Dr. <a href="/wiki/Hawley_Harvey_Crippen" title="Hawley Harvey Crippen">Harvey Crippen</a>, late 19th-century American serial killer <a href="/wiki/Herman_Mudgett" title="Herman Mudgett" class="mw-redirect">Herman Mudgett</a>, and "Unabomber" <a href="/wiki/Ted_Kaczynski" title="Ted Kaczynski">Ted Kaczynski</a>.</p>
<p>U-M athletes have starred in Major League Baseball, the <a href="/wiki/National_Football_League" title="National Football League">National Football League</a> and <a href="/wiki/National_Basketball_Association" title="National Basketball Association">National Basketball Association</a> as well as other professional sports. Notable among recent players is <a href="/wiki/Tom_Brady" title="Tom Brady">Tom Brady</a> of the <a href="/wiki/New_England_Patriots" title="New England Patriots">New England Patriots</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Famous_UM_alumni_153-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Famous_UM_alumni-153"><span>[</span>154<span>]</span></a></sup> Three players have won college football's <a href="/wiki/Heisman_Trophy" title="Heisman Trophy">Heisman Trophy</a>, awarded to the player considered the best in the nation: <a href="/wiki/Tom_Harmon" title="Tom Harmon">Tom Harmon</a> (1940), <a href="/wiki/Desmond_Howard" title="Desmond Howard">Desmond Howard</a> (1991) and <a href="/wiki/Charles_Woodson" title="Charles Woodson">Charles Woodson</a> (1997).<sup id="cite_ref-Heisman_134-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Heisman-134"><span>[</span>135<span>]</span></a></sup> Professional golfer <a href="/wiki/John_Schroeder_(golfer)" title="John Schroeder (golfer)">John Schroeder</a> and Olympic swimmer <a href="/wiki/Michael_Phelps" title="Michael Phelps">Michael Phelps</a> also attended the University of Michigan, with the latter studying Sports Marketing and Management. Phelps also swam competitively for Club Wolverine, a swimming club associated with the university.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162"><span>[</span>163<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/NHL" title="NHL" class="mw-redirect">NHL</a> players <a href="/wiki/Marty_Turco" title="Marty Turco">Marty Turco</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chris_Summers" title="Chris Summers">Chris Summers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brendan_Morrison" title="Brendan Morrison">Brendan Morrison</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jack_Johnson_(ice_hockey)" title="Jack Johnson (ice hockey)">Jack Johnson</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Michael_Cammalleri" title="Michael Cammalleri">Michael Cammalleri</a> all played for U-M's ice hockey team. <a href="/wiki/Barry_Larkin" title="Barry Larkin">Barry Larkin</a> of the Cincinnati Reds played baseball at the university. <a href="/wiki/Derek_Jeter" title="Derek Jeter">Derek Jeter</a> received a baseball scholarship to U-M, but was signed and called up by the New York Yankees before he could play there. In 2011, <a href="/wiki/Meryl_Davis" title="Meryl Davis">Meryl Davis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Charlie_White_(figure_skater)" title="Charlie White (figure skater)">Charlie White</a> win the first gold medal awarded to the United States in ice dancing in the world championship.</p>
<p>The university claims the only alumni association with a chapter on the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">moon</a>, established in 1971 when the crew of <a href="/wiki/Apollo_15" title="Apollo 15">Apollo 15</a> placed a charter plaque for a new U-M Alumni Association on the lunar surface.<sup id="cite_ref-Famous_UM_alumni_153-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Famous_UM_alumni-153"><span>[</span>154<span>]</span></a></sup> The plaque reads: “The Alumni Association of The University of Michigan. Charter Number One. This is to certify that The University of Michigan Club of The Moon is a duly constituted unit of the Alumni Association and entitled to all the rights and privileges under the Association’s Constitution.” According to the Apollo 15 astronauts, several small U-M flags were brought on the mission. The presence of a U-M flag on the moon is a long-held campus myth.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163"><span>[</span>164<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

<li id="cite_note-Rhodes-147"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Rhodes_147-0">^</a></b> Fiona Rose became the 24th Rhodes Scholar from U-M on December 6, 1997. Joseph Jewell, a U-M engineering student, became a Rhodes Scholar in 2004. Abdulrahman El-Sayed, an U-M MD/PhD student, became a Rhodes Scholar in 2008.

<td class="navbox-group" style=";;">"Worthy Runners-Up"</td>
<td style="text-align:left;border-left-width:2px;border-left-style:solid;width:100%;padding:0px;;;" class="navbox-list navbox-odd">
